**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage: Why School Should Start Later: A Wake-Up Call for Better Learning

Every morning across our nation, millions of teenagers drag themselves out of bed in the darkness, fighting exhaustion as they prepare for another early school day. This daily struggle isn't just about being tired—it's about a system that ignores basic biology and damages our young people's health, learning, and future.

The science is crystal clear. Adolescent bodies naturally fall asleep later and need more rest than adults. During the teenage years, our internal body clocks shift, making it nearly impossible to fall asleep before 11 pm. Yet most secondary schools start at 8:30 am or earlier, forcing students to wake at 6 am or before. This creates a serious sleep deficit that accumulates throughout the week.

What are the consequences of this exhaustion? Research from the Australian Sleep Health Foundation shows that sleep-deprived teenagers perform significantly worse in tests, struggle with concentration, and have higher rates of anxiety and depression. A landmark American study involving over 9,000 students found that schools starting after 8:30 am saw dramatic improvements: better grades, fewer absences, and reduced car accidents among student drivers. These aren't minor benefits—they're life-changing outcomes.

Some argue that later start times would disrupt family schedules and after-school activities. But isn't our children's health worth some adjustment? Parents already rearrange their lives for countless school requirements. Sports practices can be rescheduled. The alternative—continuing to harm our teenagers' development—is simply unacceptable.

What can be inferred about the author's view of arguments against later school start times?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) The author believes they are valid concerns that need careful consideration
2. [ ] B) The author dismisses them as less important than student health
3. [ ] C) The author agrees that family schedules cannot be changed
4. [ ] D) The author thinks sports activities are more important than sleep
5. [ ] E) The author has not considered these arguments

**Solution:**
> • The author acknowledges that some people argue later start times would disrupt family schedules and after-school activities
• The author then asks 'But isn't our children's health worth some adjustment?' and states the alternative is 'simply unacceptable'
• This rhetorical questioning and strong language shows the author considers these concerns less important than student health
• Therefore, the answer is B because the author dismisses these arguments as secondary to the health and wellbeing of students

#### Question 4 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `6ecea90a-5e0e-4aaa-9908-8f3bfd4775dc`

**Passage:**
> Walk into any school during budget planning season and you will hear the same tired argument: music programmes are luxuries we cannot afford. When money is tight, music classes are the first to be cut...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage: Why Every Student Deserves a Musical Education

Walk into any school during budget planning season and you will hear the same tired argument: music programmes are luxuries we cannot afford. When money is tight, music classes are the first to be cut. This short-sighted approach is damaging our children's futures, and it must stop now.

Music education is not a luxury—it is a necessity. Research from universities across the globe consistently shows that students who study music perform better in mathematics, reading and science. Why? Because learning an instrument strengthens the brain's ability to recognise patterns, process information and solve complex problems. When a child learns to read music, they are actually training their brain to think in multiple dimensions simultaneously. These are skills that benefit every subject, every career and every aspect of life.

Yet despite this overwhelming evidence, schools continue to treat music as an optional extra. In the past decade, thousands of music programmes have disappeared from schools worldwide. Instruments gather dust in storage rooms whilst students miss out on opportunities that could transform their lives. Is this really the future we want for our children?

Consider the facts. Students involved in music programmes attend school more regularly and are less likely to drop out. They develop discipline, patience and teamwork—qualities that employers desperately seek. A recent study of business leaders found that 90% had musical training during their school years. Coincidence? Hardly.

What can be inferred about why schools cut music programmes first when budgets are tight?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) Schools believe music programmes are the most expensive to run
2. [ ] B) Schools view music programmes as less essential than other subjects
3. [ ] C) Schools want to punish students who do not perform well academically
4. [ ] D) Schools prefer to invest in sporting programmes instead
5. [ ] E) Schools lack qualified music teachers

**Solution:**
> • The passage states that during budget planning, the 'same tired argument' is heard: music programmes are 'luxuries we cannot afford'
• The passage also mentions that schools 'continue to treat music as an optional extra' despite evidence of its benefits
• The author calls this approach 'short-sighted', indicating schools prioritise other subjects over music
• Therefore, the answer is B because schools view music as less essential or necessary compared to other subjects, treating it as a luxury that can be eliminated when money is tight

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "Why Every Student Deserves a Musical Education" (2945 chars)

### ACER Scholarship (Year 7 Entry)/practice_1 - Humanities - Sequencing & Text Organization - multiple_choice

**Total Questions in DB:** 2
**Sampled Questions:** 2

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `0aff74aa-d936-4c4e-b220-81d97ef6f928`

**Passage:**
> Every morning across our nation, millions of teenagers drag themselves out of bed in the darkness, fighting exhaustion as they prepare for another early school day. This daily struggle isn't just abou...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

The author presents the argument for later school start times in a structured way. Which option correctly shows the order in which these main ideas appear in the passage?

1. Examples of schools that successfully changed their start times
2. The biological reasons why teenagers struggle with early mornings
3. The negative effects of sleep deprivation on students
4. A description of teenagers waking up exhausted each morning

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 1, 2, 3 & 4
2. [ ] B) 4, 2, 3 & 1
3. [ ] C) 2, 4, 3 & 1
4. [ ] D) 4, 3, 2 & 1
5. [ ] E) 2, 3, 4 & 1

**Solution:**
> • The passage opens with a description of teenagers dragging themselves out of bed in darkness (4)
• Next, it explains the science behind adolescent body clocks shifting and making it hard to sleep before 11 pm (2)
• Then it discusses the consequences of exhaustion, including worse test performance and higher anxiety (3)
• Finally, it provides examples of Seattle and other schools that successfully changed their start times (1)
• Therefore, the answer is B because it correctly sequences these four main ideas as 4, 2, 3 & 1

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "Why School Should Start Later: A Wake-Up Call for Better Learning" (2801 chars)

Based on the passage 'Why Every Student Deserves a Musical Education', choose the option that lists the correct sequence in which the author presents these main ideas:

1. Music programmes have been disappearing from schools
2. Budget planning season brings arguments against music programmes
3. Music education develops qualities that employers seek
4. We must prioritise music education in every school

A) 2, 1, 3, 4
B) 1, 2, 4, 3
C) 2, 3, 1, 4
D) 4, 2, 1, 3
E) 3, 2, 1, 4

5. [ ] E) 3, 2, 1, 4

• Then discusses how music programmes have been disappearing over the past decade (idea 1 - third paragraph)
• Next presents the point about music developing discipline and qualities employers seek (idea 3 - fourth paragraph)
• Finally concludes with the call to prioritise music education in every school (idea 4 - seventh paragraph)

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `4c599d8e-3f44-4035-b0fd-7ce4596f4141`

**Passage:**
> This powerful photograph captures over 10,000 women marching down Fifth Avenue, New York, in October 1917. They carried banners demanding the right to vote. Notice their formal clothing—dresses, hats,...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
Votes for Women: The 1917 Suffrage March
This powerful photograph captures over 10,000 women marching down Fifth Avenue, New York, in October 1917. They carried banners demanding the right to vote. Notice their formal clothing—dresses, hats, and gloves—chosen deliberately to appear respectable and challenge claims that suffragettes were unladylike troublemakers. The organised rows and serious expressions show determination and discipline. Within three years, American women won voting rights.

The passage states that the women's clothing was chosen 'deliberately'. In this context, 'deliberately' DOES NOT mean:

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) intentionally
2. [ ] B) accidentally
3. [ ] C) purposefully
4. [ ] D) consciously
5. [ ] E) carefully

**Solution:**
> • The passage explains that the women chose their formal clothing 'deliberately to appear respectable and challenge claims that suffragettes were unladylike troublemakers'.
• This shows the clothing choice was planned and intentional, not random or by chance.
• 'Deliberately' means done consciously and intentionally, with purpose and care.
• Therefore, the answer is B because 'accidentally' is the opposite of deliberately—it means something done by chance without planning, which does NOT match the meaning of deliberately.

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "Votes for Women: The 1917 Suffrage March" (455 chars)

> When a honeybee finds a patch of flowers rich in nectar, it faces an important challenge. How can it tell thousands of other bees in the hive exactly where to fly? Amazingly, bees have developed a spe...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

The passage demonstrates that bee communication is primarily valuable because it:

A) Allows bees to learn improved dancing techniques from experienced colony members
B) Enables efficient food collection by sharing exact locations rather than random searching
C) Helps bees detect the sun's position even through cloud cover
D) Permits bees to recognize flower scents carried by dancing bees
E) Shows that communication in nature doesn't require human speech

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) Allows bees to learn improved dancing techniques from experienced colony members
2. [ ] B) Enables efficient food collection by sharing exact locations rather than random searching
3. [ ] C) Helps bees detect the sun's position even through cloud cover
4. [ ] D) Permits bees to recognize flower scents carried by dancing bees
5. [ ] E) Shows that communication in nature doesn't require human speech

**Solution:**

• The key message is that bee communication solves the practical challenge of efficiently feeding a colony of 20,000-80,000 bees by eliminating random searching.
• Therefore, the answer is B because it identifies the primary value and central theme: efficient resource collection through precise information sharing.

### ACER Scholarship (Year 7 Entry)/practice_2 - Humanities - Literal Comprehension - multiple_choice

**Total Questions in DB:** 6
**Sampled Questions:** 5

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `19beec4e-07f4-4d72-bed0-657a2011eb3c`

**Passage:**
> When a honeybee finds a patch of flowers rich in nectar, it faces an important challenge. How can it tell thousands of other bees in the hive exactly where to fly? Amazingly, bees have developed a spe...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
How Bees Talk Without Words
When a honeybee finds a patch of flowers rich in nectar, it faces an important challenge. How can it tell thousands of other bees in the hive exactly where to fly? Amazingly, bees have developed a special dance language that shares precise directions with their colony.

The most famous bee communication is called the waggle dance. When a worker bee returns to the hive after finding food, it performs this dance on the vertical honeycomb. The bee walks in a figure-eight pattern, waggling its body vigorously in the middle section. Other bees gather around closely, touching the dancer with their antennae to understand the message.

The dance contains two crucial pieces of information: distance and direction. The length of time the bee waggles tells other bees how far they must fly. A short waggle means the flowers are nearby, perhaps only 50 metres away. A longer waggle indicates the food source is farther, possibly up to several kilometres from the hive. Scientists have discovered that bees are remarkably accurate in their distance measurements.

Direction is even more fascinating. Bees use the sun as their compass. If the bee waggles straight up the honeycomb, it means other bees should fly directly toward the sun. If the dance points downward, they should fly away from the sun. An angle to the left or right tells bees to fly at that exact angle relative to the sun's position. This system works even on cloudy days because bees can detect the sun's location through cloud cover.

Bees also communicate through smell and sound. When a dancing bee returns from flowers, it carries the scent of those particular plants on its body. Other bees smell this scent during the dance, helping them recognise the correct flowers when they arrive. The bee also produces buzzing sounds at specific frequencies, which may convey information about the quality of the food source.

This communication system is essential for the survival of the colony. A single hive contains between 20,000 and 80,000 bees, and they must work together efficiently to collect enough nectar and pollen. Without the waggle dance, bees would waste valuable energy searching randomly for food. Instead, they can fly directly to productive locations.

Scientists first decoded the waggle dance in the 1940s, but researchers continue to discover new details about bee communication. Recent studies show that bees can even learn improved dancing techniques from experienced bees, suggesting their language is more sophisticated than previously thought. Understanding how bees share information helps us appreciate these tiny insects and their complex social behaviour. It also reminds us that communication takes many forms in the natural world, not just human speech.

Scientists first decoded the waggle dance in the:

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 1920s
2. [ ] B) 1940s
3. [ ] C) 1950s
4. [ ] D) 1970s

**Solution:**
> • The passage explicitly states: 'Scientists first decoded the waggle dance in the 1940s'
• This information is found in the second-to-last paragraph
• Therefore, the answer is B because the passage clearly identifies the 1940s as when scientists first decoded the waggle dance

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "How Bees Talk Without Words" (2749 chars)

---

#### Question 3 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `53d34884-97eb-4de1-8904-268f7b30d254`

**Passage:**
> In 1974, farmers digging a well in China made an incredible discovery. They found pieces of clay pottery that turned out to be part of an enormous underground army. This army was made of terracotta, a...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
The Mystery of the Terracotta Army
In 1974, farmers digging a well in China made an incredible discovery. They found pieces of clay pottery that turned out to be part of an enormous underground army. This army was made of terracotta, a type of baked clay, and had been buried for over 2,000 years.

The Terracotta Army was created to protect China's first emperor, Qin Shi Huang, in the afterlife. Emperor Qin ruled from 221 to 210 BCE and united China's warring states into one empire. He believed that he would need protection after death, just as he did during his life. To prepare for this, he ordered the construction of thousands of life-sized clay soldiers.

Archaeologists have uncovered more than 8,000 terracotta figures so far. The army includes soldiers, horses, chariots, and even musicians. Each soldier is unique, with different facial features, hairstyles, and expressions. Some soldiers are kneeling with crossbows, while others are standing ready for battle. The tallest figures are the generals, who can be identified by their elaborate uniforms and headgear.

The creation of this army was an enormous task. Historians estimate that over 700,000 workers spent nearly 40 years building the emperor's tomb complex. Craftsmen used local clay to mould each figure's body parts separately. Arms, legs, and heads were made individually and then attached together. After assembly, the figures were fired in large kilns at high temperatures to make them hard and durable.

Originally, the terracotta soldiers were not grey as they appear today. Scientific tests have revealed traces of bright paint on the figures. The soldiers once wore colourful uniforms in shades of red, blue, green, and purple. Their faces had realistic skin tones, and their hair and beards were black. Unfortunately, when the figures were exposed to air after excavation, the paint quickly dried and flaked off within minutes.

The Terracotta Army was arranged in military formation inside three large pits. The main pit contains about 6,000 soldiers arranged in rows. The second pit holds cavalry units and war chariots. The third pit appears to be the command headquarters, with high-ranking officers. Wooden beams once covered the pits, creating underground chambers, but these beams collapsed over time.

Today, the site is a museum that attracts millions of visitors each year. Archaeologists continue to excavate carefully, using modern technology to preserve the figures better. They now spray special chemicals on newly uncovered soldiers to protect any remaining paint. Many parts of the tomb complex remain unexcavated, and experts believe more discoveries await beneath the ground. The Terracotta Army stands as a remarkable example of ancient Chinese art, engineering, and beliefs about life after death.

How many terracotta figures have archaeologists uncovered so far?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) About 6,000 figures
2. [ ] B) More than 8,000 figures
3. [ ] C) Over 700,000 figures
4. [ ] D) Three large figures

**Solution:**
> • The passage states in paragraph 3: 'Archaeologists have uncovered more than 8,000 terracotta figures so far.'
• This is explicitly stated information that directly answers the question.
• Therefore, the answer is B because the passage clearly states that more than 8,000 figures have been uncovered.

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "The Mystery of the Terracotta Army" (2767 chars)

---

In 1974, farmers digging a well in China made an incredible discovery. They found pieces of clay pottery that turned out to be part of an enormous underground army. This army was made of terracotta, a type of baked clay, and had been buried for over 2,000 years.

The Terracotta Army was created to protect China's first emperor, Qin Shi Huang, in the afterlife. Emperor Qin ruled from 221 to 210 BCE and united China's warring states into one empire. He believed that he would need protection after death, just as he did during his life. To prepare for this, he ordered the construction of thousands of life-sized clay soldiers.

Archaeologists have uncovered more than 8,000 terracotta figures so far. The army includes soldiers, horses, chariots, and even musicians. Each soldier is unique, with different facial features, hairstyles, and expressions. Some soldiers are kneeling with crossbows, while others are standing ready for battle. The tallest figures are the generals, who can be identified by their elaborate uniforms and headgear.

The creation of this army was an enormous task. Historians estimate that over 700,000 workers spent nearly 40 years building the emperor's tomb complex. Craftsmen used local clay to mould each figure's body parts separately. Arms, legs, and heads were made individually and then attached together. After assembly, the figures were fired in large kilns at high temperatures to make them hard and durable.

Originally, the terracotta soldiers were not grey as they appear today. Scientific tests have revealed traces of bright paint on the figures. The soldiers once wore colourful uniforms in shades of red, blue, green, and purple. Their faces had realistic skin tones, and their hair and beards were black. Unfortunately, when the figures were exposed to air after excavation, the paint quickly dried and flaked off within minutes.

The Terracotta Army was arranged in military formation inside three large pits. The main pit contains about 6,000 soldiers arranged in rows. The second pit holds cavalry units and war chariots. The third pit appears to be the command headquarters, with high-ranking officers. Wooden beams once covered the pits, creating underground chambers, but these beams collapsed over time.

Today, the site is a museum that attracts millions of visitors each year. Archaeologists continue to excavate carefully, using modern technology to preserve the figures better. They now spray special chemicals on newly uncovered soldiers to protect any remaining paint. Many parts of the tomb complex remain unexcavated, and experts believe more discoveries await beneath the ground. The Terracotta Army stands as a remarkable example of ancient Chinese art, engineering, and beliefs about life after death.

The Terracotta Army was discovered in 1974 by:
A) archaeologists
B) farmers
C) historians
D) museum workers
E) soldiers

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) archaeologists
2. [ ] B) farmers
3. [ ] C) historians
4. [ ] D) museum workers
5. [ ] E) soldiers

**Solution:**
> • The first paragraph explicitly states: 'In 1974, farmers digging a well in China made an incredible discovery.'
• The passage clearly identifies farmers as the people who discovered the Terracotta Army.
• Therefore, the answer is B because the passage directly states that farmers made the discovery while digging a well.

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "The Mystery of the Terracotta Army" (2767 chars)

### ACER Scholarship (Year 7 Entry)/practice_2 - Humanities - Inference & Interpretation - multiple_choice

**Total Questions in DB:** 5
**Sampled Questions:** 5

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `e9889649-7ca4-4909-b189-7fd51fa33fcc`

**Passage:**
> Every morning, millions of teenagers around the world drag themselves out of bed in the dark, fighting against their own biology to make it to school on time. This daily struggle is not laziness—it is...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
Why Every Student Deserves a Later School Start Time
Every morning, millions of teenagers around the world drag themselves out of bed in the dark, fighting against their own biology to make it to school on time. This daily struggle is not laziness—it is science. Schools must start later to protect the health and success of their students.

The evidence is overwhelming. Research from sleep scientists shows that teenage brains are wired differently from adult brains. During adolescence, our internal body clocks shift forward by up to two hours. This means that asking a thirteen-year-old to fall asleep at nine o'clock is like asking an adult to sleep at seven o'clock. It simply does not work. Yet schools continue to ring the bell at eight-thirty or even earlier, forcing students to wake up when their bodies are still producing sleep hormones.

What are the consequences of this mismatch? Students arrive at school exhausted, unable to concentrate or learn effectively. Studies across the United Kingdom, United States and Australia reveal that sleep-deprived teenagers perform significantly worse in mathematics and reading. Their grades drop, their creativity suffers, and their memory weakens. Is this really the best we can offer our young people?

The health impacts are even more alarming. Chronic sleep loss in teenagers increases rates of depression, anxiety and obesity. It weakens immune systems, making students sick more often. Tired teenagers are also involved in more car accidents and injuries. These are not minor inconveniences—they are serious threats to wellbeing.

Some critics argue that later start times would disrupt family schedules or sporting activities. However, dozens of schools that have made the change report positive results across the board. In Seattle, when high schools shifted their start time from seven-fifty to eight-forty-five, students gained an average of thirty-four minutes of sleep per night. Attendance improved, grades rose, and tardiness decreased. Teachers reported more alert, engaged students. The benefits clearly outweigh the temporary adjustment challenges.

Others claim that teenagers should simply go to bed earlier. This argument ignores biological reality. We cannot command our bodies to sleep any more than we can command ourselves to grow taller. Teenagers are not choosing to stay awake—their brains are following a natural developmental pattern.

Every student deserves the opportunity to learn at their best. We provide nutritious meals, safe buildings and qualified teachers. Why would we not also provide appropriate schedules that match how young bodies actually work? The research is clear, the solutions are proven, and the stakes are high. It is time for schools to put student health first and move their clocks forward. Our teenagers are worth it.

Why does the writer compare falling asleep to growing taller?
A) To show that both processes happen during adolescence
B) To explain that sleep patterns cannot be controlled by willpower alone
C) To prove that teenagers need more sleep than adults
D) To suggest that sleep is important for physical growth

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) To show that both processes happen during adolescence
2. [ ] B) To explain that sleep patterns cannot be controlled by willpower alone
3. [ ] C) To prove that teenagers need more sleep than adults
4. [ ] D) To suggest that sleep is important for physical growth

**Solution:**
> • The passage states 'We cannot command our bodies to sleep any more than we can command ourselves to grow taller'
• This comparison is made in response to critics who say teenagers should simply go to bed earlier
• The writer uses growing taller as an example of something we cannot control through choice or willpower
• Therefore, the answer is B because the comparison illustrates that sleep timing, like height, is governed by biological processes beyond voluntary control

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "Why Every Student Deserves a Later School Start Time" (2777 chars)

#### Question 2 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `1ba46496-c9f5-4ff7-afe4-ff11fae7e9e5`

**Passage:**
> Amara pressed her nose against the cold window of the train, watching the English countryside blur into shades of green and grey. Three months ago, she had been in Lagos, surrounded by the bright colo...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
The Colour of Courage
Amara pressed her nose against the cold window of the train, watching the English countryside blur into shades of green and grey. Three months ago, she had been in Lagos, surrounded by the bright colours and warm voices of home. Now, she was heading to Millbrook Grammar School, where she would be the only Nigerian girl in Year Seven.

"You'll do brilliantly," her mother had said at the station that morning, straightening Amara's new uniform. But her mother's eyes had looked worried, and that worry now sat heavy in Amara's stomach.

The school hall buzzed with excited chatter as students reunited after the summer holidays. Amara stood near the door, clutching her timetable. Groups of girls laughed together, their ponytails swinging as they hugged. Nobody looked her way.

"Lost?" A voice startled her. A red-haired girl with freckles smiled kindly. "I'm Sophie. You're new, right?"

Amara nodded, relief flooding through her. "I'm Amara. I just moved from Nigeria."

"Brilliant! Come sit with us at lunch."

But at lunch, everything changed. Sophie's friends, Maya and Charlotte, whispered to each other when Amara approached. Maya wrinkled her nose at Amara's lunch box.

"What is that smell?" Maya said loudly. "It's like... weird spices or something."

Amara looked down at her jollof rice, her grandmother's recipe. The tomatoes and peppers gleamed red in the fluorescent light. Back home, everyone loved this dish. Here, it suddenly felt wrong.

"It's just rice," Amara mumbled, closing the lid.

"Why can't you eat normal food?" Charlotte added, giggling.

Sophie said nothing, but she didn't meet Amara's eyes either.

For two weeks, Amara brought plain sandwiches to school. She spoke less in class, even though she knew the answers. She tried to make herself smaller, quieter, less different.

Then came the International Day assembly. Each student could share something from their culture. Amara planned to skip it, but her English teacher, Mrs. Patterson, had other ideas.

"You have stories worth telling, Amara," Mrs. Patterson said gently. "Don't hide your light."

On the morning of the assembly, Amara's hands shook as she walked to the front. She had brought her grandmother's wrapper cloth, patterned with bold oranges and blues. She had also brought jollof rice for everyone to taste.

"In Nigeria, we say that food is love made visible," Amara began, her voice barely above a whisper. Then she remembered her grandmother's laugh, her mother's proud face at the station, and Mrs. Patterson's words. She lifted her chin and spoke louder. "This dish is called jollof rice. Every family has their own special way of making it. This is my grandmother's recipe, and she taught my mother, who taught me."

She talked about Lagos, about her grandmother's kitchen filled with laughter, about the colours and music of home. As she spoke, something shifted. Students leaned forward, listening. When she offered samples of the rice, hands shot up eagerly.

"This is amazing!" someone called out.

After the assembly, Maya approached hesitantly. "I'm sorry about what I said before. That was really mean. Your lunch looked different, and I... I didn't understand. But different isn't bad."

Amara considered the apology. Maya looked genuinely sorry, her cheeks pink with embarrassment.

"Thank you," Amara said finally. "Maybe next time, you could ask about things instead of judging them."

Maya nodded seriously. "I will. I promise."

That afternoon, Amara opened her lunch box without hesitation. The jollof rice smelled like home, like love, like courage. And this time, when others looked her way, she smiled back.

Why did Amara bring plain sandwiches to school for two weeks?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) She wanted to try different English foods
2. [ ] B) She felt ashamed after her food was criticized
3. [ ] C) Her grandmother's recipe was too difficult to make
4. [ ] D) The school did not allow students to bring rice
5. [ ] E) She preferred sandwiches to jollof rice

**Solution:**
> • The passage shows that Maya and Charlotte criticized Amara's jollof rice, saying it smelled weird and asking why she couldn't eat 'normal food'
• After this incident, the passage states 'For two weeks, Amara brought plain sandwiches to school. She spoke less in class, even though she knew the answers. She tried to make herself smaller, quieter, less different.'
• This behavior indicates Amara was trying to fit in and avoid further criticism by hiding her cultural differences
• Therefore, the answer is B because Amara felt ashamed after her food was criticized and wanted to avoid being different

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "The Colour of Courage" (3635 chars)

#### Question 4 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `ee1376c8-d8ba-4bc8-9192-6accfef66002`

**Passage:**
> Walk down any beach in the world and you will see them. Plastic bags tangled in seaweed. Plastic bags floating in the shallows. Plastic bags buried in the sand. These flimsy carriers, used for mere mi...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
Why Our Cities Must Ban Single-Use Plastic Bags Now
Walk down any beach in the world and you will see them. Plastic bags tangled in seaweed. Plastic bags floating in the shallows. Plastic bags buried in the sand. These flimsy carriers, used for mere minutes, remain in our environment for centuries. It is time for every city to ban single-use plastic bags completely.

Some people argue that plastic bags are convenient and cheap. They claim that shoppers need them to carry groceries home. But this argument ignores a simple truth: we managed perfectly well before plastic bags existed. Our grandparents used cloth bags and baskets. They survived. We can too.

The environmental damage caused by plastic bags is staggering. Each year, Australians use approximately 3.6 billion plastic bags. Most end up in landfill or, worse, in our oceans. Marine animals mistake these bags for food. Sea turtles think they are jellyfish and eat them, often dying as a result. Birds feed plastic fragments to their chicks. Is our convenience worth the death of innocent creatures?

Critics say that banning plastic bags will hurt businesses and inconvenience customers. Yet cities that have already implemented bans prove otherwise. Rwanda banned plastic bags in 2008 and is now one of Africa's cleanest nations. Bangladesh banned them in 2002 after discovering they blocked drainage systems and caused flooding. In Australia, major supermarkets stopped providing single-use plastic bags in 2018. Customers adapted within weeks. Businesses thrived. Life continued.

The solution is remarkably simple. Reusable bags cost just a few dollars and last for years. They are stronger than plastic bags and can carry heavier loads. Many fold into tiny pouches that fit in pockets or handbags. Once you develop the habit of bringing your own bag, it becomes second nature. The minor inconvenience of remembering a bag is nothing compared to the environmental catastrophe we currently face.

Some argue we should focus on recycling instead of banning. However, recycling plastic bags is difficult and expensive. Most recycling facilities cannot process them. They jam machinery and contaminate other recyclables. Only a tiny percentage of plastic bags are actually recycled. The rest persist in our environment for up to 1,000 years.

We stand at a critical moment. Future generations will judge us by the choices we make today. Will we continue sacrificing our oceans and wildlife for a few minutes of convenience? Or will we show the courage to change our habits? The answer should be obvious. Every city, every town, every community must ban single-use plastic bags immediately. Our planet cannot wait any longer. The time for action is now.

What can be inferred about the author's view on people's ability to adapt to a plastic bag ban?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) People will find it too difficult to change their shopping habits
2. [ ] B) People can successfully adjust to using alternatives within a short time
3. [ ] C) People need government support to make the transition
4. [ ] D) People prefer plastic bags over all other options
5. [ ] E) People will stop shopping at supermarkets

**Solution:**
> • The passage states that when Australian supermarkets stopped providing plastic bags in 2018, 'Customers adapted within weeks'
• The author also mentions that 'Once you develop the habit of bringing your own bag, it becomes second nature'
• The author references how grandparents managed with cloth bags and baskets, showing people can adapt
• Therefore, the answer is B because the author clearly believes people can successfully adjust to alternatives in a short period of time

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "Why Our Cities Must Ban Single-Use Plastic Bags Now" (2668 chars)

What can be inferred about the author's view of critics who suggest teenagers should simply go to bed earlier?
A) The author believes they have not considered the scientific evidence
B) The author thinks they are concerned about family schedules
C) The author agrees that teenagers stay awake by choice
D) The author feels they understand teenage sleep patterns

**Options:**

2. [ ] B) The author thinks they are concerned about family schedules
3. [ ] C) The author agrees that teenagers stay awake by choice

• The author compares commanding sleep to commanding growth—both impossible
• The author emphasizes that teenagers follow a 'natural developmental pattern' and are not choosing to stay awake

The passage contrasts teenage sleep patterns with adult sleep patterns by:
A) Showing that teenagers need more hours of total sleep than adults

#### Question 5 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `f3ac92bf-f29e-4cdf-8e4c-eef5aa2124ec`

**Passage:**
> Amara pressed her nose against the cold window of the train, watching the English countryside blur into shades of green and grey. Three months ago, she had been in Lagos, surrounded by the bright colo...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
The Colour of Courage
Amara pressed her nose against the cold window of the train, watching the English countryside blur into shades of green and grey. Three months ago, she had been in Lagos, surrounded by the bright colours and warm voices of home. Now, she was heading to Millbrook Grammar School, where she would be the only Nigerian girl in Year Seven.

"You'll do brilliantly," her mother had said at the station that morning, straightening Amara's new uniform. But her mother's eyes had looked worried, and that worry now sat heavy in Amara's stomach.

The school hall buzzed with excited chatter as students reunited after the summer holidays. Amara stood near the door, clutching her timetable. Groups of girls laughed together, their ponytails swinging as they hugged. Nobody looked her way.

"Lost?" A voice startled her. A red-haired girl with freckles smiled kindly. "I'm Sophie. You're new, right?"

Amara nodded, relief flooding through her. "I'm Amara. I just moved from Nigeria."

"Brilliant! Come sit with us at lunch."

But at lunch, everything changed. Sophie's friends, Maya and Charlotte, whispered to each other when Amara approached. Maya wrinkled her nose at Amara's lunch box.

"What is that smell?" Maya said loudly. "It's like... weird spices or something."

Amara looked down at her jollof rice, her grandmother's recipe. The tomatoes and peppers gleamed red in the fluorescent light. Back home, everyone loved this dish. Here, it suddenly felt wrong.

"It's just rice," Amara mumbled, closing the lid.

"Why can't you eat normal food?" Charlotte added, giggling.

Sophie said nothing, but she didn't meet Amara's eyes either.

For two weeks, Amara brought plain sandwiches to school. She spoke less in class, even though she knew the answers. She tried to make herself smaller, quieter, less different.

Then came the International Day assembly. Each student could share something from their culture. Amara planned to skip it, but her English teacher, Mrs. Patterson, had other ideas.

"You have stories worth telling, Amara," Mrs. Patterson said gently. "Don't hide your light."

On the morning of the assembly, Amara's hands shook as she walked to the front. She had brought her grandmother's wrapper cloth, patterned with bold oranges and blues. She had also brought jollof rice for everyone to taste.

"In Nigeria, we say that food is love made visible," Amara began, her voice barely above a whisper. Then she remembered her grandmother's laugh, her mother's proud face at the station, and Mrs. Patterson's words. She lifted her chin and spoke louder. "This dish is called jollof rice. Every family has their own special way of making it. This is my grandmother's recipe, and she taught my mother, who taught me."

She talked about Lagos, about her grandmother's kitchen filled with laughter, about the colours and music of home. As she spoke, something shifted. Students leaned forward, listening. When she offered samples of the rice, hands shot up eagerly.

"This is amazing!" someone called out.

After the assembly, Maya approached hesitantly. "I'm sorry about what I said before. That was really mean. Your lunch looked different, and I... I didn't understand. But different isn't bad."

Amara considered the apology. Maya looked genuinely sorry, her cheeks pink with embarrassment.

"Thank you," Amara said finally. "Maybe next time, you could ask about things instead of judging them."

Maya nodded seriously. "I will. I promise."

That afternoon, Amara opened her lunch box without hesitation. The jollof rice smelled like home, like love, like courage. And this time, when others looked her way, she smiled back.

How did Amara's behaviour change between the lunch incident and the International Day assembly?
A) She became more withdrawn and then more confident
B) She became more talkative and then more quiet
C) She became more angry and then more forgiving
D) She became more friendly and then more distant

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) She became more withdrawn and then more confident
2. [ ] B) She became more talkative and then more quiet
3. [ ] C) She became more angry and then more forgiving
4. [ ] D) She became more friendly and then more distant

**Solution:**
> • After the lunch incident, the passage states: 'For two weeks, Amara brought plain sandwiches to school. She spoke less in class, even though she knew the answers. She tried to make herself smaller, quieter, less different.' This shows she became more withdrawn.
• During the International Day assembly, Amara 'lifted her chin and spoke louder' and shared her culture confidently, with students responding positively. This shows she became more confident.
• Therefore, the answer is A because the passage explicitly describes Amara's transformation from withdrawn behaviour (speaking less, making herself smaller) to confident behaviour (speaking louder, sharing her culture openly).

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "The Colour of Courage" (3635 chars)

> Walk down any beach in the world and you will see them. Plastic bags tangled in seaweed. Plastic bags floating in the shallows. Plastic bags buried in the sand. These flimsy carriers, used for mere mi...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

The author presents the argument for banning plastic bags by discussing different aspects in a particular order. Which sequence correctly shows how the author organized the main points?

1. Examples of cities that successfully banned plastic bags
2. Description of plastic bags polluting beaches
3. The simple solution of using reusable bags
4. Why recycling plastic bags doesn't work

A) 2, 1, 3, 4
B) 1, 2, 4, 3
C) 2, 3, 1, 4
D) 2, 4, 1, 3
E) 1, 3, 2, 4

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 2, 1, 3, 4
2. [ ] B) 1, 2, 4, 3
3. [ ] C) 2, 3, 1, 4
4. [ ] D) 2, 4, 1, 3
5. [ ] E) 1, 3, 2, 4

**Solution:**
> • The passage opens with a description of plastic bags polluting beaches (paragraph 1): "Walk down any beach..." (2)
• The author then addresses critics by providing examples of cities that successfully banned plastic bags (paragraph 4): "Rwanda banned plastic bags..." (1)
• Next, the author presents the simple solution of using reusable bags (paragraph 5): "Reusable bags cost just a few dollars..." (3)
• Finally, the author explains why recycling doesn't work (paragraph 6): "However, recycling plastic bags is difficult..." (4)
• Therefore, the answer is A because the correct sequence is 2, 1, 3, 4

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "Why Our Cities Must Ban Single-Use Plastic Bags Now" (2668 chars)

Based on your understanding of the passage, choose the option that lists the correct sequence of ideas as presented by the author:
1. Schools that changed start times report positive results
2. Teenagers drag themselves out of bed fighting their biology
3. Sleep-deprived students perform worse academically
4. Teenage body clocks shift forward during adolescence

A) 2, 4, 3, 1
B) 4, 2, 3, 1
C) 2, 3, 4, 1
D) 1, 2, 4, 3
E) 4, 3, 1, 2

1. [ ] A) 2, 4, 3, 1
2. [ ] B) 4, 2, 3, 1
3. [ ] C) 2, 3, 4, 1
4. [ ] D) 1, 2, 4, 3
5. [ ] E) 4, 3, 1, 2

**Solution:**

• The second paragraph then explains the science behind this: teenage body clocks shift forward during adolescence (4)
• The third paragraph discusses the consequences: sleep-deprived students perform worse academically (3)
• The fifth paragraph presents evidence that schools that changed start times report positive results (1)
• Therefore, the answer is A because it follows the exact sequence in which these ideas appear in the passage: 2, 4, 3, 1

#### Question 2 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `4e23da62-6468-4c8c-8dae-0365e0015f95`

**Passage:**
> High in the mountains of South America, an incredible network of roads once connected a vast empire. The Inca civilisation built over 40,000 kilometres of roads across some of the world's most challen...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage:
The Ancient Incan Road System: Connecting an Empire
High in the mountains of South America, an incredible network of roads once connected a vast empire. The Inca civilisation built over 40,000 kilometres of roads across some of the world's most challenging terrain. This remarkable achievement linked their empire from modern-day Colombia to Chile, crossing deserts, mountains, and rainforests.

The Incan road system, called Qhapaq Ñan, was constructed without the use of wheels or iron tools. Workers used stone hammers and bronze chisels to carve paths through solid rock. In flat areas, roads were up to 15 metres wide. In mountainous regions, they became narrow stone staircases climbing steep slopes. Some sections even included rope bridges that swung across deep valleys.

These roads served many important purposes for the empire. Messengers called chasquis ran along the routes, carrying news and information between cities. They could travel up to 240 kilometres in a single day by passing messages to the next runner at relay stations. The roads also allowed armies to move quickly across the empire, helping the Inca maintain control over distant regions. Additionally, traders used the routes to transport goods like food, textiles, and precious metals.

The Inca built their roads with careful planning and impressive engineering skills. In swampy areas, workers created raised causeways to keep the road surface dry. Through deserts, they placed marker posts to guide travellers when sand covered the path. In the mountains, they constructed drainage systems to prevent rainwater from washing away the roads. Stone retaining walls supported roads built on steep hillsides.

Rest houses called tambos were positioned every 20 to 30 kilometres along major routes. These buildings provided shelter, food, and supplies for official travellers. Storage facilities at tambos held emergency food reserves for times of famine. Some tambos were simple structures, whilst others were elaborate complexes with multiple buildings.

The road system reflected the Inca's strong central government and organised society. Only people on official business received permission to use the main roads. Common citizens needed special approval to travel. This control helped the empire manage resources and maintain security.

Today, sections of the ancient Incan roads still exist. Some routes remain in use by local communities in Peru, Bolivia, and Ecuador. In 2014, UNESCO recognised Qhapaq Ñan as a World Heritage Site, acknowledging its historical importance. Archaeologists continue to study these roads to understand how the Inca built and maintained such an extensive network without modern technology. The roads stand as a testament to the ingenuity and determination of this ancient civilisation.

The Incan messengers were called:
A) tambos
B) chasquis
C) Qhapaq Ñan
D) causeways

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) tambos
2. [ ] B) chasquis
3. [ ] C) Qhapaq Ñan
4. [ ] D) causeways

**Solution:**
> • The passage states: 'Messengers called chasquis ran along the routes, carrying news and information between cities.'
• This directly identifies the name given to Incan messengers.
• Therefore, the answer is B because the passage explicitly states that messengers were called chasquis.

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "The Ancient Incan Road System: Connecting an Empire" (2749 chars)

Karl von Frisch's research on honeybees is significant primarily because it:

A) Proved that honeybees can fly several kilometres to find food
B) Revealed that insects can possess sophisticated communication systems

4. [ ] D) Demonstrated that bees can dance in figure-eight patterns
5. [ ] E) Explained why bee populations need to be protected for crop pollination

**Solution:**

• The final paragraph emphasizes the key message: 'The waggle dance demonstrates that complex communication is not limited to humans' and that this discovery 'helped scientists appreciate the intelligence of insects'
• While options A, C, D, and E describe specific details mentioned in the passage, the central significance of von Frisch's work was revealing that insects possess sophisticated communication abilities
• Therefore, the answer is B because the passage's main theme focuses on how this research changed scientific understanding of insect intelligence and communication complexity

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

What is the main purpose of the waggle dance performed by honeybees?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) To entertain other bees in the hive
2. [ ] B) To communicate the location of food sources to other bees
3. [ ] C) To show which bee is the strongest in the colony
4. [ ] D) To warn other bees about dangerous predators
5. [ ] E) To help bees practice their flying skills

**Solution:**
> • The passage opens by explaining that when a honeybee finds flowers with nectar, it needs to tell other bees where to find the food
• The waggle dance is introduced as the answer to this communication challenge
• Throughout the passage, details explain how the dance conveys direction, distance, and quality of food sources
• Therefore, the answer is B because the main purpose of the waggle dance is to communicate the location of food sources to other bees in the hive

> Read the passage and answer the question.

The passage states that the Incan road system was a 'remarkable achievement'. In this context, the word 'remarkable' most closely means:

A) ordinary
B) extraordinary
C) difficult
D) ancient
E) useful

**Solution:**

• It was built without wheels or iron tools, which shows it was an impressive accomplishment.
• 'Remarkable' means worthy of attention or extraordinary, not ordinary (the opposite), and while the roads were difficult to build, ancient, and useful, these words don't capture the meaning of 'remarkable' in this context.

Title: Votes for Women: A 1915 Suffrage Poster
This bold poster from New Zealand shows a woman in working clothes holding a ballot paper. The text reads: "Women Won the Vote Here in 1893 — Why Not You?" It was distributed in Britain during the suffrage movement. The image uses striking colours — purple, green and white — the official colours of the Women's Social and Political Union. The confident stance and direct gaze challenge viewers to take action for equality.

The passage states that the poster was 'distributed' in Britain. In this context, 'distributed' means:

A) destroyed
B) spread around
C) collected
D) hidden

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) destroyed
2. [ ] B) spread around
3. [ ] C) collected
4. [ ] D) hidden

**Solution:**

• In this context, 'distributed' means the poster was given out or spread around to people in Britain
• The purpose was to share the message of the suffrage movement with a wider audience
• Therefore, the answer is B because 'distributed' means spread around or given out to many people

**Passage:**
> Maya pressed her nose against the cold glass, watching the storm rage across the grey sea. Lightning split the sky, and thunder rattled the lighthouse windows. She had lived here all her life, perched...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

What can be inferred about why Maya's father shared his own childhood dreams with her?

A) He wanted to convince Maya to stay at the lighthouse
B) He wanted Maya to understand he empathized with her feelings
C) He regretted not leaving the island when he was young
D) He believed Maya would fail at boarding school
E) He was trying to make Maya feel guilty about leaving

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) He wanted to convince Maya to stay at the lighthouse
2. [ ] B) He wanted Maya to understand he empathized with her feelings
3. [ ] C) He regretted not leaving the island when he was young

5. [ ] E) He was trying to make Maya feel guilty about leaving

• He shares this personal experience immediately after Maya expresses her fears and desires about leaving
• By revealing he once had similar dreams, he is showing Maya that he can relate to her feelings
• Therefore, the answer is B because his purpose was to demonstrate empathy and understanding, not to manipulate her decision or criticize her ambitions

> Read the passage and answer the question.

Based on the passage, what does the author believe about parents' reasons for wanting their children to have smartphones at school?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) Parents have genuine safety concerns that cannot be addressed otherwise
2. [ ] B) Parents are making excuses because they themselves cannot disconnect
3. [ ] C) Parents need to contact children frequently for legitimate reasons
4. [ ] D) Parents want their children to have access to educational tools
5. [ ] E) Parents are unaware of the dangers smartphones pose

**Solution:**
> • The passage states: 'Defenders of smartphone access often claim that parents need to contact their children during school hours. But let's be honest—how did families manage perfectly well for generations before mobile phones existed?'
• The author then says: 'This argument is simply an excuse masking our own inability to disconnect.'
• The author is directly stating that parents' stated reasons are not the real reasons—they are excuses covering up parents' own addiction or inability to be without constant contact.
• Therefore, the answer is B because the author believes parents are making excuses to hide their own inability to disconnect from their devices.

#### Question 4 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `92cba4bd-97bc-4df8-b161-76ccc97ad220`

**Passage:**
> Walk into any school cafeteria today and you'll see something alarming: bins overflowing with uneaten vegetables whilst students line up for chips and sugary snacks. Is it any wonder that childhood ob...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

Passage: Why Every School Needs a Vegetable Garden

Walk into any school cafeteria today and you'll see something alarming: bins overflowing with uneaten vegetables whilst students line up for chips and sugary snacks. Is it any wonder that childhood obesity rates have tripled in the past thirty years? The solution is simpler than you might think, and it's growing right beneath our feet.

Every school in Australia should be required to maintain a vegetable garden where students grow, harvest and eat their own produce. This isn't just about healthy eating—it's about transforming how young people understand food, nature and responsibility.

Consider the evidence. Schools with vegetable gardens report that students eat 40% more vegetables than their peers. Why? Because children who grow tomatoes, carrots and lettuce develop a genuine connection to these foods. They've watched seeds sprout, pulled weeds in the hot sun, and felt the satisfaction of harvesting something they've nurtured. These vegetables aren't mysterious items from a supermarket shelf—they're the result of their own hard work.

But the benefits extend far beyond nutrition. School gardens are outdoor classrooms where mathematics, science and environmental studies come alive. Students measure garden beds, calculate growing seasons, observe insects and decomposition, and understand ecosystems firsthand. They learn that food doesn't magically appear in shops; it requires soil, water, time and care. This knowledge is disappearing from our society, and we're paying the price.

Critics argue that schools lack the space, time and money for gardens. These objections crumble under scrutiny. Even small courtyards can accommodate raised beds or vertical gardens. Garden maintenance requires just thirty minutes weekly, easily incorporated into existing science or health lessons. As for cost, initial setup expenses are minimal—soil, seeds and basic tools—whilst ongoing costs are virtually zero. Compare this to expensive sports equipment that schools replace annually.

Some educators worry about curriculum pressure, claiming there's no time for gardening when students must prepare for tests. This thinking is precisely backwards. Gardens don't distract from learning; they enhance it. Students retain information better when they're engaged in hands-on activities. A child who's planted beans understands plant biology far better than one who's merely read about it.

The question isn't whether we can afford school gardens. It's whether we can afford not to have them. With childhood diabetes rates soaring and environmental awareness more critical than ever, school gardens offer a practical, affordable solution. They teach students to value fresh food, understand where it comes from, and develop skills they'll use for life.

Isn't it time we gave every child the chance to get their hands dirty and discover that vegetables can actually be exciting? The evidence is clear, the benefits are numerous, and the cost is negligible. School gardens aren't a luxury—they're a necessity for the health and education of our children.

What can be inferred about why students currently waste vegetables in school cafeterias?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) The vegetables are not fresh enough
2. [ ] B) Students lack a personal connection to the vegetables they are served
3. [ ] C) School cafeterias serve too many vegetables
4. [ ] D) Students prefer chips because they are cheaper
5. [ ] E) The vegetables are not properly cooked

**Solution:**
> • The passage states that children who grow vegetables develop a genuine connection to these foods and that these vegetables aren't mysterious items from a supermarket shelf
• It contrasts this with the current situation where bins overflow with uneaten vegetables
• The implication is that students currently waste vegetables because they lack the personal connection that comes from growing them
• Therefore, the answer is B because students don't have a relationship with vegetables they haven't grown themselves, making them less likely to eat them

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "Why Every School Needs a Vegetable Garden" (3061 chars)

Why do students in schools with vegetable gardens eat more vegetables than other students?
A) Because vegetables from gardens taste better than store-bought ones
B) Because they feel personally connected to the food they have grown
C) Because their teachers force them to eat what they grow
D) Because garden vegetables are healthier than supermarket vegetables

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

According to the passage, the author contrasts smartphones with computers and tablets in schools based on:
A) their cost
B) their educational value
C) the level of supervision and access control
D) their popularity among students

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) their cost
2. [ ] B) their educational value
3. [ ] C) the level of supervision and access control
4. [ ] D) their popularity among students

**Solution:**
> • The passage states that 'Primary schools already have computers and tablets for educational purposes, used under teacher supervision with appropriate software.'
• In contrast, the passage says 'Personal smartphones, however, grant unrestricted access to social media, games and inappropriate content.'
• The key difference identified is that computers/tablets are supervised with appropriate software (controlled access), while smartphones provide unrestricted access.
• Therefore, the answer is C because the author contrasts them based on supervision and access control, not cost, educational value, or popularity.

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `b27a823b-bd9a-44a3-b806-2566ff4a44dd`

**Passage:**
> Walk into any primary school playground today and you'll see something deeply troubling. Instead of children running, laughing and playing together, many sit hunched over screens, thumbs scrolling end...

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

The author presents the argument against smartphones in primary schools by organizing different types of evidence. Which option correctly shows the order in which these main points appear in the passage?

1. The negative impact on social skills and face-to-face interactions
2. Evidence of harm to children's mental health and brain development
3. Observation of children's behavior in school playgrounds
4. Examples of successful smartphone bans in other countries

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 1, 2, 3 & 4
2. [ ] B) 3, 2, 1 & 4
3. [ ] C) 2, 3, 4 & 1
4. [ ] D) 3, 4, 2 & 1
5. [ ] E) 4, 3, 2 & 1

**Solution:**
> • The passage opens with an observation about children in school playgrounds hunched over screens (3)
• The second paragraph presents research evidence about harm to memory, thinking, anxiety and depression (2)
• The fifth paragraph discusses social damage including cyberbullying and missed opportunities for real friendships (1)
• The sixth paragraph provides examples of France's successful smartphone ban (4)
• Therefore, the answer is B because the sequence is 3, 2, 1 & 4

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "It's Time to Ban Smartphones in Primary Schools" (3187 chars)

### ACER Scholarship (Year 7 Entry)/practice_3 - Humanities - Poetry Analysis - multiple_choice

**Total Questions in DB:** 3
**Sampled Questions:** 3

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `1e9bd7ee-4c1b-43f2-bc45-b80ecf849416`

**Passage:**
> The cobblestones remember every footstep,
Worn smooth by centuries of hurried feet.
Vendors call out like songbirds at dawn,
Their voices weaving through the morning heat.

Beneath striped awnings, co...

**Question:**
> Read the poem 'The Old Market Square' and answer the question.

Title: The Old Market Square

The cobblestones remember every footstep,
Worn smooth by centuries of hurried feet.
Vendors call out like songbirds at dawn,
Their voices weaving through the morning heat.

Beneath striped awnings, colours collide—
Crimson tomatoes, golden loaves of bread,
Silver fish gleaming on beds of ice,
Purple plums piled high like treasure spread.

The butcher's knife goes chop-chop-chop,
Rhythm steady as a beating drum.
Children dart between the crowded stalls,
Their laughter bright as they skip and run.

An old woman sits beside her flowers,
Her hands like branches, gnarled and brown.
She ties each bouquet with patient care,
A queen of petals in this bustling town.

The fountain whispers secrets to the square,
Its water dancing in the midday sun.
Generations meet here, young and old,
Where countless stories merge into one.

As shadows lengthen, the market sighs,
The vendors pack their wares away.
But tomorrow the square will wake again,
Ready to greet another vibrant day.

What does the old woman do in the market?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) She sells fish on beds of ice
2. [ ] B) She ties bouquets with patient care
3. [ ] C) She calls out like a songbird
4. [ ] D) She dances by the fountain
5. [ ] E) She sells loaves of bread

**Solution:**
> • The poem describes an old woman sitting beside her flowers
• The text states 'She ties each bouquet with patient care'
• This is an explicit detail about her activity in the market
• Therefore, the answer is B because the poem directly tells us she ties bouquets with patient care

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "The Old Market Square" (978 chars)

---

#### Question 2 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `cbab1d45-9a05-4a69-aaf1-1d8822a24831`

**Passage:**
> The cobblestones remember every footstep,
Worn smooth by centuries of hurried feet.
Vendors call out like songbirds at dawn,
Their voices weaving through the morning heat.

Beneath striped awnings, co...

**Question:**
> Read the poem 'The Old Market Square' and answer the question.

[Full poem text as provided]

What poetic device is used in the line 'Vendors call out like songbirds at dawn'?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) Metaphor
2. [ ] B) Simile
3. [ ] C) Personification
4. [ ] D) Alliteration
5. [ ] E) Rhyme

**Solution:**
> • The line 'Vendors call out like songbirds at dawn' compares the vendors' voices to songbirds using the word 'like'
• A simile is a comparison between two things using 'like' or 'as'
• A metaphor would make a direct comparison without 'like' or 'as', and personification gives human qualities to non-human things
• Therefore, the answer is B because the line uses 'like' to create a comparison, which is the defining feature of a simile

**Notes:**
- ℹ️ Passage: "The Old Market Square" (978 chars)

---

**Question:**
> Read the passage and answer the question.

What is the central message of this passage about Thonis-Heracleion?

A) Ancient cities can be rediscovered using modern technology
B) A lost Egyptian city was found underwater, teaching us about the past and the power of nature
C) Earthquakes and flooding destroyed an important port city
D) Archaeologists found statues and ships on the ocean floor

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) Ancient cities can be rediscovered using modern technology
2. [ ] B) A lost Egyptian city was found underwater, teaching us about the past and the power of nature
3. [ ] C) Earthquakes and flooding destroyed an important port city
4. [ ] D) Archaeologists found statues and ships on the ocean floor

• The final paragraph explicitly states the central message: the discovery 'has taught historians valuable lessons about ancient trade and culture' and 'reminds us that even great civilisations can vanish' due to natural forces

A) Individual bees would not know how to perform the waggle dance
B) The thousands of bees could not coordinate their activities and work together
C) Queen bees would be unable to produce the necessary pheromones
D) Flowers would be too far away for bees to find on their own
E) Scientists would not be able to study bee behavior

### ACER Scholarship (Year 7 Entry)/practice_5 - Mathematics - Probability - multiple_choice

**Total Questions in DB:** 5
**Sampled Questions:** 5

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `f7606174-ebef-408a-8acc-fb5c5e0881b3`

**Question:**
> A weather station recorded 40 days of weather: 15 sunny days, 18 cloudy days, and 7 rainy days. If you randomly select one day from this record, what is the probability that it was a sunny day?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) fifteen chances in twenty-five
2. [ ] B) fifteen chances in forty
3. [ ] C) three chances in eight
4. [ ] D) fifteen chances in thirty-three
5. [ ] E) three chances in seven

**Solution:**
> • Total number of days recorded = 40 days
• Number of sunny days = 15 days
• Probability of selecting a sunny day = 15/40
• Simplifying the fraction: 15/40 = 3/8
• Therefore, the answer is C because the probability is three chances in eight

#### Question 2 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `e1129a7f-06d0-4de5-be57-d49502e5efa5`

**Question:**
> A computer lab has 30 tablets available for students: 18 tablets are fully charged and 12 tablets are partially charged. During morning classes, 3 fully charged tablets were borrowed and not returned. At lunchtime, a teacher randomly selects 2 tablets from those remaining for a presentation. What is the probability that both tablets selected are fully charged?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 35/117
2. [ ] B) 105/351
3. [ ] C) 5/13
4. [ ] D) 15/27
5. [ ] E) 9/26

**Solution:**
> • After 3 fully charged tablets are borrowed, there are 15 fully charged tablets and 12 partially charged tablets remaining (27 tablets total)
• The probability of selecting the first fully charged tablet is 15/27
• After selecting one fully charged tablet, there are 14 fully charged tablets left out of 26 total tablets
• The probability of selecting the second fully charged tablet is 14/26
• The probability that both are fully charged is (15/27) × (14/26) = 210/702 = 105/351
• Therefore, the answer is B because the compound probability of two dependent events requires multiplying the individual probabilities, accounting for the change in totals after each selection

#### Question 3 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `238cc799-113d-42dc-8a11-688a4d5e65db`

**Question:**
> A music festival has three stages performing simultaneously. Stage A performs for 40 minutes then takes a 20-minute break (repeating this pattern). Stage B performs for 30 minutes then takes a 30-minute break (repeating this pattern). If both stages start performing at the same time, and a visitor arrives at a random time during the first 2 hours, what is the probability that both stages are performing when the visitor arrives?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 1/3
2. [ ] B) 1/6
3. [ ] C) 1/4
4. [ ] D) 1/2
5. [ ] E) 2/3

**Solution:**
> • Stage A cycle: 40 min performing + 20 min break = 60 min total. In 120 minutes, Stage A performs during: 0-40, 60-100 min (80 minutes total).
• Stage B cycle: 30 min performing + 30 min break = 60 min total. In 120 minutes, Stage B performs during: 0-30, 60-90 min (60 minutes total).
• Both stages perform simultaneously during: 0-30 min (overlap of 0-40 and 0-30) and 60-90 min (overlap of 60-100 and 60-90) = 30 + 30 = 40 minutes total.
• Probability = 40/120 = 1/3.
• Therefore, the answer is A because both stages are performing together for 40 out of 120 minutes.

---

#### Question 4 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `7cf4dd26-9c83-4735-8bbe-d535791ad570`

**Question:**
> A museum gift shop has a lucky dip box containing 16 small prizes: 6 keychains, 5 magnets, and 5 bookmarks. A visitor picks one prize without looking and keeps it. Then another visitor picks a second prize without looking. What is the probability that both prizes picked are keychains?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 6 chances in 16
2. [ ] B) 1 chance in 8
3. [ ] C) 3 chances in 16
4. [ ] D) 1 chance in 4
5. [ ] E) 6 chances in 15

**Solution:**
> • First pick: Probability of picking a keychain = 6/16
• After first keychain is removed: 5 keychains remain out of 15 total prizes
• Second pick: Probability of picking a keychain = 5/15
• Probability both are keychains = (6/16) × (5/15) = 30/240 = 1/8
• Therefore, the answer is B because the probability of both prizes being keychains is 1 chance in 8

### ACER Scholarship (Year 7 Entry)/practice_5 - Mathematics - Geometry - Perimeter & Area - multiple_choice

**Total Questions in DB:** 5
**Sampled Questions:** 5

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `41b11208-53a4-4a57-b655-42ea976e5a1a`

**Question:**
> A rectangular vegetable patch has a width of 5 metres and a perimeter of 26 metres. What is the area of the vegetable patch?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 30 square metres
2. [ ] B) 40 square metres
3. [ ] C) 45 square metres
4. [ ] D) 50 square metres
5. [ ] E) 65 square metres

**Solution:**
> • Perimeter of rectangle = 2(length + width)
• 26 = 2(length + 5)
• 13 = length + 5
• Length = 8 metres
• Area = length × width = 8 × 5 = 40 square metres
• Therefore, the answer is B because the area of the vegetable patch is 40 square metres

---

#### Question 2 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `030c53ea-2c37-4ed8-9b99-64730677f018`

**Question:**
> A cycling track forms a rectangle with rounded ends (semicircles). The straight sections are each 80 metres long, and the total perimeter of the track is 400 metres. What is the diameter of each semicircular end?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 30 metres
2. [ ] B) 38.2 metres
3. [ ] C) 40 metres
4. [ ] D) 60 metres
5. [ ] E) 76.4 metres

**Solution:**
> • The track has two straight sections of 80 metres each, totaling 160 metres
• The remaining perimeter consists of two semicircular ends, which together form one complete circle
• Perimeter of the circular portion = 400 - 160 = 240 metres
• Using the circumference formula: πd = 240, where d is the diameter
• Therefore d = 240 ÷ π ≈ 240 ÷ 3.14159 ≈ 76.4 metres
• Therefore, the answer is E because the diameter of each semicircular end is approximately 76.4 metres

---

#### Question 3 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `66f96c3c-6f7f-4045-ac90-4a2f95e09ce0`

**Question:**
> A rectangular chess board has a perimeter of 112 cm. If the length of the board is 32 cm, what is the area of the chess board?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 672 sq cm
2. [ ] B) 768 sq cm
3. [ ] C) 864 sq cm
4. [ ] D) 896 sq cm
5. [ ] E) 1024 sq cm

**Solution:**
> • Perimeter = 2(length + width), so 112 = 2(32 + width)
• 112 = 64 + 2(width), so 48 = 2(width)
• Width = 24 cm
• Area = length × width = 32 × 24 = 768 sq cm
• Therefore, the answer is B because the area of the chess board is 768 square centimetres

2. [ ] B) 0.4

4. [ ] D) 2.5
5. [ ] E) 4.0

#### Question 3 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `31a9ca9f-da90-40dd-a9c1-453cff31e110`

**Question:**
> A drone's battery level is displayed on a power indicator from 0 to 3 units. The drone starts a flight at 2 and 1/4 units. After flying, the battery drops to 5/6 units. The drone then recharges to exactly halfway between its starting level and its lowest level. What is the battery level after recharging?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 1 and 1/12 units
2. [ ] B) 1 and 13/24 units
3. [ ] C) 1 and 7/12 units
4. [ ] D) 1 and 3/4 units
5. [ ] E) 2 units

**Solution:**
> • Starting level: 2 and 1/4 = 9/4 units
• Lowest level: 5/6 units
• Find halfway point: (9/4 + 5/6) ÷ 2
• Convert to common denominator: 9/4 = 27/12 and 5/6 = 10/12
• Sum: 27/12 + 10/12 = 37/12
• Halfway: 37/12 ÷ 2 = 37/24 = 1 and 13/24 units
• Therefore, the answer is B because the battery level after recharging to the halfway point is 1 and 13/24 units

---

#### Question 4 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `7a637381-5da1-4ea4-b6ce-fee638645c31`

**Question:**
> A solar panel's energy output is displayed on a meter from 0 to 5 units. In the morning, the output is at 1 and 2/5 units. By midday, the output increases to 3 and 4/5 units. What is the increase in energy output?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 2 and 1/5 units
2. [ ] B) 2 and 2/5 units
3. [ ] C) 2 and 3/5 units
4. [ ] D) 3 and 2/5 units
5. [ ] E) 5 and 1/5 units

**Solution:**
> • Convert mixed numbers to improper fractions: Morning = 1 and 2/5 = 7/5 units, Midday = 3 and 4/5 = 19/5 units
• Calculate the difference: 19/5 - 7/5 = 12/5 units
• Convert back to mixed number: 12/5 = 2 and 2/5 units
• Therefore, the answer is B because the increase in energy output is 2 and 2/5 units

### ACER Scholarship (Year 7 Entry)/practice_5 - Mathematics - Logic Puzzles & Algebraic Reasoning - multiple_choice

**Total Questions in DB:** 4
**Sampled Questions:** 4

#### Question 1 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `c7f71d6b-c270-42e2-8533-66f4b7dceac9`

**Question:**
> At an archaeology site, researchers classify artifacts by age codes. They discover that 5 pottery shards plus 3 arrowheads have a combined code value of 41. They also find that 2 pottery shards plus 5 arrowheads have a combined code value of 38. What is the code value of one pottery shard?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 5
2. [ ] B) 7
3. [ ] C) 8
4. [ ] D) 9
5. [ ] E) 11

**Solution:**
> • Let P = pottery shard value and A = arrowhead value
• First equation: 5P + 3A = 41
• Second equation: 2P + 5A = 38
• Multiply first equation by 5: 25P + 15A = 205
• Multiply second equation by 3: 6P + 15A = 114
• Subtract second from first: (25P + 15A) - (6P + 15A) = 205 - 114
• This gives: 19P = 91, so P = 91 ÷ 19 = 7
• Check: If P = 7, then from 5P + 3A = 41: 35 + 3A = 41, so 3A = 6, thus A = 2
• Verify with second equation: 2(7) + 5(2) = 14 + 10 = 38 ✓
• Therefore, the answer is B because one pottery shard has a code value of 7

---

#### Question 2 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `66a17feb-34ff-4a91-ae3f-928b3e07e820`

**Question:**
> At a space station, cargo containers are weighed in units. The crew discovers that 2 fuel pods plus 4 supply crates weigh 36 units, while 3 fuel pods plus 1 supply crate weigh 29 units. What is the weight of one fuel pod?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 6 units
2. [ ] B) 8 units
3. [ ] C) 10 units
4. [ ] D) 12 units
5. [ ] E) 14 units

**Solution:**
> • Let F = fuel pod weight and S = supply crate weight
• First equation: 2F + 4S = 36
• Second equation: 3F + 1S = 29
• From the second equation: S = 29 - 3F
• Substitute into first equation: 2F + 4(29 - 3F) = 36
• Simplify: 2F + 116 - 12F = 36
• Combine like terms: -10F + 116 = 36
• Solve: -10F = -80, so F = 8
• Check: If F = 8, then S = 29 - 3(8) = 5. First: 2(8) + 4(5) = 16 + 20 = 36 ✓ Second: 3(8) + 1(5) = 24 + 5 = 29 ✓
• Therefore, the answer is B because one fuel pod weighs 8 units

---

#### Question 3 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `1bb27728-2fce-4f48-ac64-57a79b3a8f5b`

**Question:**
> At a pet shop, the owner weighs different animals. She finds that 2 rabbits weigh the same as 1 cat. If one cat weighs 8 kilograms, how much does one rabbit weigh?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 2 kilograms
2. [ ] B) 4 kilograms
3. [ ] C) 8 kilograms
4. [ ] D) 10 kilograms
5. [ ] E) 16 kilograms

**Solution:**
> • We know that 2 rabbits = 1 cat
• We are told that 1 cat weighs 8 kilograms
• Therefore, 2 rabbits = 8 kilograms
• To find the weight of 1 rabbit, we divide: 8 ÷ 2 = 4 kilograms
• Therefore, the answer is B because one rabbit weighs 4 kilograms

#### Question 4 ✅ (PASS)

**ID:** `2318b4a8-ac9f-49b4-8040-ac8bdc2de986`

**Question:**
> At a sports equipment store, tennis balls and golf balls are sorted by weight. The manager finds that 4 tennis balls weigh the same as 3 golf balls. If one golf ball weighs 45 grams, how much does one tennis ball weigh?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) 30 grams
2. [ ] B) 33.75 grams
3. [ ] C) 36 grams
4. [ ] D) 40 grams
5. [ ] E) 60 grams

**Solution:**
> • If 4 tennis balls = 3 golf balls, and 1 golf ball = 45 grams
• Then 3 golf balls = 3 × 45 = 135 grams
• So 4 tennis balls = 135 grams
• Therefore 1 tennis ball = 135 ÷ 4 = 33.75 grams
• Therefore, the answer is B because one tennis ball weighs 33.75 grams

|------------|--------|---------|-----------|----------|
| Vegetables | 45     | 38      | 52        | 41       |

How many seedlings in total were planted on Tuesday and Wednesday combined?

**Question:**
> A marine aquarium tracks the feeding schedule for different sea creatures. The table shows the daily food requirements:

| Creature | Base Amount (grams) | Per kg of Body Weight (grams) |
|----------|---------------------|-------------------------------|
| Seahorse | 2 | 0.5 |
| Starfish | 3 | 1 |
| Small octopus | 5 | 2 |
| Jellyfish | 1 | 0.25 |

The aquarium has a 3 kg small octopus and a 8 kg starfish. If food costs $0.20 per gram, what is the total daily cost to feed both creatures?

**Options:**
1. [ ] A) $4.20
2. [ ] B) $4.40
3. [ ] C) $5.00
4. [ ] D) $5.40
5. [ ] E) $6.20

**Solution:**

• Starfish: 3 + (1 × 8) = 3 + 8 = 11 grams per day
• Total food needed: 11 + 11 = 22 grams per day
• Total cost: 22 × $0.20 = $4.40
• Therefore, the answer is B because the combined daily feeding cost is $4.40
